I love that Luxembourg's trains are on time. How can they not be? It is Luxembourg... üôÇ At 07:18
I realized you forgot something : rail network architecture, which inherently makes pods pointless.  The idea of rail network architecture is to find a balance between interconnection and efficiency (aka not making too many rails segments).  Imagine you need to connect 10 places together. You could interconnect each with every other. That would be 3 628 800 rails segments in total. That's too much.   So what you do, is that if three places are roughly aligned, like A-B-C, you simply connect A with B and B with C, and connection to A and C is done indirectly through B This way you have already much much less segments.  Then, you notice something : not every link sees the same traffic. A link between two very crowded places (like big cities) sees a LOT more traffic. So what you do is that you upgrade those links to handle this heavy traffic, and because more traffic means more money to invest into infrastructure. You now have strong (fast, high traffic) and weak links (slow, low traffic).   Given that, it is often better so simply make a detour to use strong links in a journey, instead of using the shortest weak link combo. What happens then is that the network is divided according to crowded places : crowded places are connected by strong links, and other places are only linked to the closest crowded place (eventually indirectly through other places) by weak links.   In the end, a rail network roughly has two levels : a "strong" net-shaped network made only of strong link, connecting crowded places, and a "weak", star-shaped network organized each crowded place.   This makes pods inherently useless :  - On weak links seeing low traffic, pods are not very useful, because they can't go fast, and the distances aren't that high anyway. So small commuter trains are fine - On strong links seeing heavy traffic (and usually high distances), pods are useless, as the heavy traffic makes large-scale high speed trains much more suited.

We had a MAGLEV "U-Bahn" (on stilts) in West-Berlin in the late 80s, but after reunification it was dismantled, because it ran over land that could have houses and shopping centers on it (the Potsdamer Platz). Though it should be noted that SNCF invested in MagRail (for the tech with maglev over rails, not the pods), while its infrastructure management division is currently looking to automate the busiest high-speed lines, especially LGV Sud-Est first, which is the oldest and busiest in Europe. They want to implement a centralized autopilot system in order to squeeze in a couple more trains per hour on the line thanks to even shorter headways which would only be possible if the speed of all the trains simultaneously running on the line is precisely known and adjusted in real-time.  The technology that's being developed by MagRail could be used on high-speed trains instead of pods, and that's what SNCF is interested in. Also, most lines opened in the last 25 years in France were built to higher speed standards, so the track geometry could allow higher speeds, especially if friction is reduced to only the pantograph.  The MagRail pod vehicles have no practical use on the French HSR network, which is eyeing 200 million passengers yearly by the end of the decade. But the MagRail tech itself could. In fact, the speed of passenger flights stagnates since multiple decades simply because we are at the threshhold where conventional jet planes would become exponentionaly less efficient with increased speed. Hence why research in passenger planes concentrate more on making more fuel efficient engines rather than going faster.

You want to be more efficient? No problem, but being more efficient while fulfilling all safety requirements? Complicated. We had once some tech bros from india who tried to sell us their train supervision system, which was basically our already existing supervision system, just without all the safety measures. They really thought they could just use their crappy coding and some ETCS subsets and get rich with it.
So for now their technology allows eg for a German steel company to reduce the transport from one stage of the plant to another from two hours to five minutes. They also have a deal with the French railway operator as with a small addition to a normal train you can turn it into a magnetic train. Because of the dent, the wheel exploded at a high speed, blowing a whole in the roof and lifting the train into the air and of the rails. The accident happened in carriage 3. You basically run your regional trains with high as fuck acceleration so they aren't in the way of your faster trains as much. Note that this technology exists and has been used already, by systems such as Skytrain in Vancouver. About French trains, iirc, I'm fairly sure the tracks can actually handle MUCH faster than 320 km/h (the max speed of a TGV in commercial use). We did manage to send one of those OVER 500km/h for a world record, but the main limiting factor isn't the tracks, it's something much dumber.  The power cables above the train, turns out that on everyday use, you need to give them some wiggle room as they will contract and expend a bit depending on temperature, and because of that slack, as a train goes, it creates this bump in front of it, where the cable is lifted slightly.  again, iirc, simulations showed that a train going at ~400km/h would be fast enough to actually catch up with that bump, and therefore lose connection with the power cables, which is a big nono

To put into perspective the actual infeasibility that minimum railway curves produce for high speeds (in case you think there is a tech solution):   Conventional Rail minimum curves (at speed) are roughly 800-1200 metres in radius. The Chuo Shinkansen maglev, which is going to operate at a max speed of 505km/h, has a minimum curve of *8,000* metres in radius.  You cannot 'upgrade' the tracks to run faster without violating the laws of physics. Track curvature increases exponentially with speed, even if you angle tracks, tilt the trains, redistribute the weight blah blah blah you can't outdo physics.

The "Transrapid Disaster" showed why self-driving trains are not a good idea. Though, to be fair, you very much focus on the top speed and "pod" design, while ignoring the actual advantages that Nevomo (supposedly) has: Higher cargo (weight) capacity, better acceleration (especially uphill), and shorter breaking distance. The interesting thing is that they can actually learn from Disney.  Years back Disney tried to put a new, fast ride called Rocket Rods, which was themed like a futuristic transportation system (which feels almost too similar to Nevomo), on their old tram tracks and it just ended up breaking the old infrastructure because it wasn't rated for those kinds of forces.  They were only able to run the ride for 2 years before they had to shut it down for good.  Even for that brief amount of time it was problematic due to using the existing infrastructure which lack of banked turns on the track.  That lack of banked turns caused the ride to have to quickly decelerate before each turn, take the turn, then accelerate up again; and all of this speeding up and slowing down caused rapid wear of the vehicle's tires as well as exacerbated the wear on the tram tracks.  The rapid wear of the tires caused engine damage which caused all manner of other reliability problems.

The biggest thing people need to understand about making trains go faster... is that the rails themselves are the barrier, not that the rails need replaced, but rather that they need to be relocated entirely. "On existing tracks" severely misunderstands the engineering problem of faster trains.  TGV already holds the record for going 550 km/h, but they don't have a single line running that speed because the tracks themselves have to be made for it.  This is the #1 reason the hyperloop is ultimately stupid, at least right now, air resistance isn't really a problem for trains, it's upgrading the rail network to support faster trains. Adding the complexity of a vacuum to travel through does effectively nothing but drive up the already prohibitive cost of HSR.

Look, maybe these tech bros just want to be able to use the train without breathing the air poor people breathe, I won't discount the idea.. but the only thing these guys are ever after is a money-making scheme, and they've been taught first that their harebrained ideas about technology are as valid as those of an expert and second that their job, essentially, is to "disrupt the market" with "innovation". They think if they can make something work *differently*, they can chisel off a portion of the market so that they are getting paid for it instead of the person who's currently getting paid for it. They need to make it better in some way in order to capture a chunk of the market. They can't make it cost less, they don't know how rail transit works, they're not experts in any of the relevant fields.  That leaves making it more "premium". A more luxurious rail experience is one option, but how do you sell that idea to more than just the bougiest of bougies? Plus, you gotta quantify something to give a "value proposition". What you can quantify and sell to randos is speed. It's relatively easy to say "here's the speed my transit can go" and get people excited. You have to back that up, so you can simplify the mechanism and demonstrate the theoretical speed capability of the thing, and just hope nobody looks too closely.  That's all I think is happening here. It's not that they've built something for themselves because they had a need, it's that they once rode a train and went "oooh, this is a powerful industry. What one thing slightly bothered me about it, and how can I steal some of this business?"

7:26 The problem with this statistic may not be obvious to Americans, but if you look at it closely, you realize that the comparison is flawed. With the exception of France, "long distance" routes are compared with each other that have nothing to do with a long distance because the countries are tiny compared to Italy or Germany. Their rail networks and their size and capacity utilization are a joke compared to Germany, and therefore much easier to manage. So it's no wonder that punctuality is better. The average deviations in Germany, by the way, are only a few minutes in most cases, but the statistics are completely distorted by individual delays lasting several hours or the complete cancellation of trains.
Plz remember: the only reason why we germans can reach those giga chat numbers is because we don't count trains that never arrive.  After a certain amount of time we cancel late trains, so they don't show up in our statistics.  Our trains are bad EVEN AFTER CHEATING!

It's astounding that the people behind this company haven't even looked at actual implementations of retrofitted HSR tracks. In Japan, most of the Shinkansen lines run on purpose-built tracks that allow for 270-320km/h, but two (Yamagata and Akita) run partly on old conventional rail tracks that were retrofitted for Shinkansen. They re-gauged the rails from narrow gauge to standard gauge and added various safety features, but even then the trains are unable to run at higher than 130km/h on the retrofitted tracks due to the problems with curvature, level crossings, noise, and interactions with slower trains, all described in this video.  As an aside, the reason Yamagata and Akita Shinkansens lines were built in spite of the slow speed is because they offer through service to Tohoku Shinkansen, which does run at 300km/h to Tokyo. Being able to travel between Yamagata or Akita and Tokyo without changing trains was considered enough of a merit, even if a part of the journey is being run at conventional rail speed. And yes, both lines are believed to be profitable.

Accelerating to 550km/hr would be a huge issue as well, steel wheels on steel track can reliably accelerate at around 1.6m/s^2 with an 8% slip rating on wet track, yet the fastest accelerating EMUs you will find in the UK are the Class 380 and 456 and they only just about hit 1m/s^2 the normal value across the UK fleets fall into the 0.55-0.75m/s^2 range. This isn't down to power or traction limitations but passenger comfort. It turns out people don't like having hot coffee spilled in their lap (ask the passengers on the ill-fated APT) or sliding off seats (lowering the jerk and joust can help a lot with comfort). If we were to take the 3 car 380's 1m/s^2 as the upper limit of passenger acceptability for acceleration and deceleration then just getting to 550km/hr will take over 7 minutes and a little under 11.7km (about 7 and a quarter miles) with the same again to stop (assuming the acceleration remains linear, this isn't normally the case).  This puts a big dent in how close you can run trains ETMS will help with this as the old track blocks disappear but that's a multi billon pound project in itself for even a relatively small country like the UK. High speed rail isn't something you can do on the cheap using existing infrastructure.

Great analysis and thanks for calling out the horrible situation here in Germany. The state of our rail system and especially the "Deutsche Bahn" has become a nation wide meme. Complaining about the DB is a national hobby :) I've seen many US expats who come to germany and praise the great public infrastructure. After a few years they stand in line and complaining about the system like every real german would :P  Politics have ruined our railway system over the last decades. Originally the whole train and railway system was in the hand of the state. In 1994 the DB was partly privatized. From that point on it just went downhill. Especially the contracts between the different parties had horrible conditions for the state. One of the most prominant issue is that the maintenance of the tracks is the duty of the now private company, however rebuilding / upgrading and expanding the railway network is state duty. Guess what, they simply didn't maintain the tracks until they were "fubar" so they had to be replaced by the state. Who would have thought that a pivate company would do everything they can to save money on the back of the people? This is a huge mess.

German here, our punctuality gets worse, because we dont count trains that never arrive in our own statistics. The DB is a fucking shitshow and the fact that apparently the US is worse than our clusterfuck of a long-distance railway system scares me.  Also, out Railway network is technically speaking privately operated, the DB (Deutsche Bahn, translated German Trains) is a private company owned 100% by the country. It has its own CEO and has to make a profit. This was implemented at the end of the 1990s if memory serves, with the reasoning being "something something free market something something competition drives innovation something something", and the problems of the "free market" aside, there is no actual competition. At time of privatisation, the DB was the only train operator in Germany, and while since then we have gotten 1 or 2 other companies running trains, they all share the same rail system because having a rail network for each company would be fucking idiotic. Arguably one of the other largest problems of our railway network is that freight trains and passager trains share the rails every now and again, similar to, as you mentioned, the long-distance and short-distance connections. It also doesnt help that the DB is actively taking away railtracks because they arent profitable, leading to worse connections, more passengers per train, and more trains per track. Swiss here. Great video! As far as I know, most of our trains COULD drive faster, but they limit max speeds everywhere except when you're far away from any cities or villages. And it's not because the tracks couldn't handle it, it's because the tracks are rarely completely walled in, so it's possible for branches, animals, and especially people, to get on the tracks. Trains don't plan on just plowing through whatever gets in their way, they actually want a chance to slow down / brake if necessary. I know some train conductor and they are getting training for specific tracks / areas in the sense of "Watch out in this specific bend / crossing, there's a high chance for cows on the tracks there" and so on.  Not too long ago we further limited the top speed in cities since there were too many instances of suicide by train. Lower top speeds in densely populated areas gives the conducters a bigger window to break in these events.   (PS: I really appreciate the usage of the Starcraft 1, Sims 2 and AoE 1 soundtracks.)

Railway employee here. I had to watch it again and make a new comment, because I cannot fathom the stupidity of this idea.  Shunting is what we call it when we move wagons around in an enclosed area (typically a station or a yard if you will), to put them together in a new config, load them up and send them off.  The shunting locomotives are typically smaller than the hauler locomotives and run on diesel engines, but they still do the job efficiently.  A train of 15-20 wagons can be put together in a short time using one such shunting locomotive.  Having individual wagons run under their own power as proposed in this video, is mind blowingly ignorant! These suggestions are super inefficient compared to todays system. üòÇ

No train or pod can ever go 550 or even 300 on regular tracks, because regular tracks are not spaced far enough from each other. And so when passing each other, the under pressure between them makes them suck each other off the tracks and crash together. This was already a known issue like a hundred years ago, and this literally used to happen. So if any vehilce could and would speed up to high speeds on a regular track, it would immediately end in a disaster.

I'm not sure your arguments are really thought through. For example about cargo freight what about things that you either can't afford to order a container per day (eg businesses operating with tight margins) or you can't anticipate demand (eg warehousing in multiple locations) or the goods are only available for shipping with short notice (eg mail) or even domestic goods (perhaps in your country domestic production isn't common but there should be at least some local production).   You should look in to train automation in other countries, it's not impossible like you're suggesting. It's just a matter of local regulations catching up to the tech. I've worked with remotely operated trains before.  Looking only at throughput will give you the wrong idea about why trains are useful. Every day millions commute by train. If you trippled the capacity but halved the speed then yes your throughput would increase, but realistically the number of people using the train would be less. Having fast trains increases the area in which people can live and still work in the city. A good example would be Beijing or Tokyo where vast areas are linked to the city by train. Most people using trains aren't going extremely long distance for pleasure, they are commuting. Smaller faster more frequent trains mean more people can commute from a larger area.  I appreciate that trains in Europe are a huge mess, but the rest of the world isn't like that. Try to consider a more global context.

Line 3 of S√£o Paulo Metro system moves more than 400 million passenger per year moving on an average speed of 32 Km/h. How they do that? With 52 big trains that can fit 280 tons of passengers (around 3.8k passengers) going every 3 minutes in each station. One of the big strengths of standard rail is how FORGIVING it is. Track subsiding? Lift it up with a forklift and dump more gravel under it. Did the nails holding the track down pop out? Send a couple guys out to smack in some new ones. Need to replace a length of track? Melt out the old one, melt in a new one. Introducing more speed and precision removes those advantages, and if you do so without making the train way more efficient, then you're wasting money and effort.

Oh, someone had an a*spain, so made up most of the "claims" Nevomo never did, LOL. They don't claim they want 550 km/h freight. They say MagRail cargo 150-200 km/h will be OK (today the fastest cargo cars are designed for max. 140 km/h). MagRail is primarily not about speed, but about less energy and silent movement (most of noise today is generated by suspended electric traction and current collectors / pantographs) without building separate railway rout (which is at least very problematic in highly urbanized areas with high share of private areas). But the best solution would be constructing a brand new HSL with geometry meant for 400+ km/h traffic, shared by both classic HST and magnetic trains. HST for international trains (because there's no other continental scale standard as ETCS). I know there's a huge classic rail lobby, but we don't need to be forcibly down-scaled by it every time and everywhere.

In France, without any levitation technology, we already have a TGV that reached a speed of 574.8 km/h between Bordeaux and Paris for a record in 2007. It's actually just a regular TGV without any kind of levitation technology.
I used to work for a company called Linear Motors Ltd which evolved from the electrical research department of famous crane maker Herbert Morris. Some one had the bright idea that instead of driving a crane along the gantry using wheels driven by an electric motor & gearbox, with a brake to stop it, let's use linear motors instead and use the steel gantry beams as a reaction plate to provide thrust to push the crane along. That saves having to buy motors and gearboxes and brakes. The synchronous speed of 6m/sec is far too fast for a crane, so then you have to add a speed controller and a speed feedback sensor to keep the speed down to a more reasonable 0.5m/sec. Then you need to come up with a means of stopping the crane, because if it's barrelling along at full speed with a load on the hook and the power is interrupted there's nothing to stop it. With a conventional brake, the brakes apply if you lose the power. Needless to say, it didn't catch on.

I will have to disagree on a few points. First off, smaller trains(aka pods) running more often isn't necessarily a bad idea. Self driving trains isn't a very hard problem to solve(or to be more exact, a remotely managed system where operators only manage oddities like Waymo). It reduces how big a station has to be allowing for more stations to be built, and reduces wait times. Effectively, this is what a tram network is.   The problem start when you try to made the pods high speed. Now there are 100s of precession machines to maintain. You need to have passing lanes which must take up space similar to a highway ramp(to avoid high g forces) meaning your stations are now huge and must accommodate more people then fit in a pod. Since high speed is only useful between cities, you already need larger capacities then a single pod even if you were running one every 5 minutes; trams work because they avoid ever concentrating people, but high speed rail is starting with concentrated people.

I don't get the whole obsession with "pods", it's not really a good idea anywhere, and especially not for trains. Trains are long for a reason, it's called economy of scale.  Regular train tracks are, as you said, not made for those kinds of speeds so that is already a non-starter for something like this. The way to do something like this would probably be on dedicated mag-lev tracks, probably put on pillars to keep people and animals out of the way. People walk across train tracks for all kinds of stupid reasons, with fatal consequences, and that's even at fairly slow speeds. Now imagine some poor idiot, or maybe a deer or an elk, getting splattered from a orange dildo doing 550kmph. It would be a bad day for everyone involved. I imagine birds would also be an issue at those kinds of speeds, they can usually get out of the way of a train but this might be too fast for them to react properly in time.  The idea of self driving container cars is something that could be viable in some places, and I think it's already being done in container ports with trucks, but there is no point whatsoever in doing it with mag-lev. If you need something fast you get it by a truck that can get it all the way from point A to B, unless you happen to have a straight train connection from your supplier to the factory. If you want something quickly from China, you pay extra for air freight.   The concept is interesting but not viable for so many reasons and practically impossible without dedicated infrastructure. Even though trains are very punctual, compared to other countries, my problem with them is price.  If you want to go from amsterdam to the other end of the country, Winterswijk, it'll cost ‚Ç¨28,17 for a single journey.  A return costing two singles ‚Ç¨56,34.  That cost scales liniarly per travel companion.  So if you and your 3 mates want a daytrip to Amsterdam collectively you'll shell out ‚Ç¨225.36  The same distance by car, 162km, will cost you ‚Ç¨20,52, assuming at least 15km per liter of gasoline, and a liter costing ‚Ç¨1,9. Meaning the round trip costs you ‚Ç¨41,04.  Now if you go with your 3 mates the cost doesnt scale. is still ‚Ç¨41,04.  Yes you need to park, which will set you back about ‚Ç¨25 so now the total Journey cost is, ‚Ç¨66,04.  Thats way too big of a price difference for it to make sense.  The whole point of public transport should be to reduce journey cost, especially when travelling in groups!  Even in going by yourself the price difference is only ‚Ç¨10 in favour of trains, thats only 20% cheaper. Not enough to incentivise people to take the train instead of car.  Especially if you take into account that driving a lighter vehicle by yourself will be more in range of 20km per liter and that you can find cheaper spots to park a bit further out. I know that there are other costs to the car, maintenance, depreciation, insurance and road tax that we didnt take into account, but you dont have enough consistent data to spread those costs out per KM, unlike rail companies who have loads of usage data that should be fairly consistent. However, generally you wouldnt own a car unless you need it to get to work.  So you will be fine paying for those fixed costs already so i dont think its justified to add them in this journey cost.

Tech-bros are obsessed with the idea "faster = better", but let me tell you a tale why that isn't always the case and why plans to use the Transrapid maglev train that's even faster than the ICE in Germany were canceled as inefficient.  Back in 1981, France opened their first high-speed Paris-to-Lyon rail line for their TGV, which became a financial success. Naturally, we Germans wanted our own sleek marvel of train engineering: The first German Intercity Express (ICE) ran in 1991. Why had it taken 10 years of development, planning, and construction time? Because German engineers & city planners did the math beforehand and realized: - With the ICE potentially (more on that later) able to reach speeds of 330 km/h, they had to build entirely new rail track beds that could withstand stronger vibrations. -  The higher the speed the straighter the route has to be or you risk losing the train in a curve, hence entirely new routes and tracks had to be planned and laid in many places.  - Higher speed = more windshear, hence existing train tracks were too close together for an ICE to pass another train safely.  But most importantly, something came up which had nothing to do with engineering but _distances:_ France is bigger than Germany, also Germany is more densely populated and its cities are closer together. *Oftentimes, by the time the German ICE has reached its maximum speed it already needs to brake and slow down again as it approaches its next stop!*  Unless you want to build high-speed routes that solely go North-South from Berlin or Hamburg to Munich (Bavaria) or Rome (Italy), or from Paris (France) to Brussels (Belgium) to the Ruhrpott urban megaplex in western Germany and east to Prague (capital of Poland) or Warsaw (capital of Czech Republic), _with absolutely no stops in between for many hundreds of kilometers_ and on which only the ICE is allowed to run, the ICE can't even fully utilize its speed within Germany. (ICE is still faster than the standard trains that connect smaller towns to cities, but YKNWIM.) And as mentioned in the video, different European countries use different rail track distances for historical reasons.   Hence why the plan of using the even faster Transrapid maglev train in Germany was canned. What would be the point? Instead we Europeans sold the Transrapid concept to nations like China which have vastly larger distances between its ever growing urban population centers.
Slow trains or no german automobiles?  Easy choice.

I work as an assistant for a company specialized in software running on trains, and the modern techbros have absolutely no idea just how many failsafes, regulations, interconnected systems and plain ol' human ingenuity is required to manufacture a good train control system. Autonomous driving trains are still strongly coupled to local or remote controls not because they're obsolete, but because while humans are fallible, they're also pretty good at spotting problems way before they can arise on the track. Don't get me wrong, having an automated system for smooth braking and following cruise speeds under speed limits is great, but there's a reason why all non-developing countries have heaps of manuals of human-controlled subsystems required to get the choo-choos running.

Zero margin for safety aside, the power requirements are absolutely absurd, the dominant majority of energy losses from air friction over the flat parallel surfaces alone, but only since the train has to be several cars long to have enough traction to fight the nose and tail drag.

High speed rail is a good way forward but it also has to deal with many problems: you need new to integrate the tracks into the topography of the terrain, the existing cities, towns and villages and the already existing infrastructure, like highways and other rails as well as thousands of regular roads. It is not an easy task to build a high speed train station in an existing city without putting the station too far from the city nor having to demolish a strip of land inside the city. Many countries, especially in Europe, have a landscape with high density network of towns and villages with very little to no option how to draw a straight line though it. As said in the video, highers speeds requiere less turns and the lower the number of possible turns the higher the difficulty to fit it beteween existing buldings and traffic. You can get rid of the houses  by demolishing them but that requires forced evictions and destruction of entire communities - and those people will fight back. And there are buldings you cannot demolish like historical monuments... and most villages in my country (Czechia) have at least one.   The current speed l is very often limited not not by the available technology, but by all the things that are in the way of the proposed rail, the burreaucratic difficulties of such a complex project and the extremely high cost of construction. We need to build high speed rails nevertheless, it is worth the costs. But the technology already exists, we just need to find the way how to realise it. Such a project is always about finding the equilibrium between the efficiency on one hand and the cost to the enviroment, humans and the wallets on other.   The proposed "invention" do not solve any of these problems but create bunch of new.  And every sane person know you cannot drive 500km/h on the existing rails. That is just not physically posible.

I think the greatest improvement for any rail would be maximizing the frequency.  I mean, if I have to wait 30 mins on a train, then ride it for another 30 mins, then shortening my wait time by half will have greater impact than increasing speed from 160 to 240km/h.  In general doubling max speed will not cut the time in two, cause of the stops and time needed to slow down and accelerate - so its diminishing returns.   So it seems that unifying and improving control systems is the way to go both as a step towards automation and decrasing minimum intervals between trains.   If we achieve automation, then probably trains will get smaller (more trains per hour might mean less seats per train are needed) but more frequent, or at least I think this would be best when increasing the convenience of travel.

It's not just delays from the slower trains that are the problem. The more important reason is that if you have a high-speed service on a mixed line you actually get fewer train paths because the high speed train even when running to timetable forces everything else to get out of its way. So, it brings down the number of passenger journeys possible and increases the overall journey time for most journeys. Rail Natter has good videos explaining this. Also, slabtrack use it not just about ballast flight. The damage caused to the ballast from the forces on high-speed lines means you would have to be continually maintaining it. So, slabtrack is cheaper / easier to use. It's also more consistent in holding track geometry, which is needed at higher speeds.

yeah, this system is idiotic, but the idea of pods isn't if the infrastructure can meet 2 basic requirements: * achieve full automation * each station is either a terminal or a bypass.  on such a system you could run "virtual trains" where pods are linked together by software and run in unison with the added benefit that long range pods don't stop at every station. This would enable you to group passengers with long distance tickets into such pods. Of course, this means that pods on the main tracks would always run close to maximum speed, and you'd need additional acceleration and deceleration tracks at every station in the network.  The control system can then tell the pods which need to exit the stream, and a few behind to slow down a bit to ensure the necessary separation for switching, after which they'd catch up with the rest of the pods. Similarly, pods could join such a virtual train on route without the need to physically stop the train for coupling.   Such a system does offer some marginal benefits over existing railways, and it's much more suited for a maglev in tube (i.e. hyperloop) design but would require massive upfront investment in infrastructure. I wouldn't dissconsider it altogether but Poland could benefit more from investment in high speed rail at this moment in time.  I live in Romania and  at this moment in time I would be perfectly content with trains that run like the Austrian Railjets. Those things are amazing. But I won't disconsider hyperloop for a future technology because as of now, it's the only technology which can replace long range air travel without producing emissions.

I would question the need for high speed freight rail.  Why?  Generally (and I have worked in Freight Forwarding, so have some experience with this) the kinds of companies that need to transport stuff via container tend to have their freight procedures organised, so it doesn't matter so much that their freight might be on the train (or a truck) for 3 or 4 hours rather than a couple of hours.   Even where freight has a tight delivery time (such as anything that needs to be frozen or kept cold), they will have procedures in place for dealing with this, even if they need to liaise with local authorities to close roads etc.     They don't need a pod that carries their container at 500km/h..

There is this old joke: Swiss and German railways wanted to save costs and joined forces to buy platform signs. It's German for both, right? So they got together and discussed the idea for six months and finally concluded it wasn't feasible. Switzerland needed signs saying "On time" (98%), "One minute late" (1.9%), "Two minutes late" (0.09%), "Three minutes late" (0.01%), "Please hurry if you want to catch the connection" (just in case). Germany needed: "One hour late", "Two hours late", "Canceled" and "Train station out of service". They even double checked and confirmed that they would never need the "On time" sign.

Thank you: I laughed so much!
"Rolling dildo" is something i'm going to use when discussing HS2 more often.  On an aside - LIM systems are a thing but they have never been used for HSR. The main advantage of LIM is that you don't need to have motors onboard the train. This frees up capacity onboard the train and reduces track wear. Where's the best place for this system? Well, the Japanese have beaten Europe to this. There are several metro systems in Japan that use LIM to ensure that the trains are extremely light and efficient. These are metros. By using LIM, you can run an extremely regular service over a very short distance and you can get even more people onboard the train as there aren't bulky components taking up space.  Yes, LIM can also be used for maglev - but the cost is so astronomical to building a maglev system over long distances that this rarely gets beyond a planning stage or feasibility study. Yes, again Japan, is building the Chuo shinkansen, but this is primarily to compete with mid distance aviation transport over an incredibly dense route.  As for why we don't use LIM on high speed rail - it is, as you said, waaaay more expensive and the advantages gained don't outweigh the capital expenditure. It is far more efficient and effective to just have standard OLE - which you would need any way, even if you had an LIM system in the middle of the rails.  HSR is very rarely justified. Super HSR is just a non-starter. China can justify 350kph as the distances in China can just about offset the expense. But anywhere else - no. And at 350kph, the physics of rail travel start to get weird. Trains are already unstable - at higher speeds, they effectively "float" on the railhead and the trains experience an effect referred to as "hunting" where the wheels seek an equilibrium position between the tracks. The higher the speed, the greater the effect of hunting. This can be mitigated with expensive wheel designs but there is a theoretical limit. Once you exceed the limit, the train no longer "hunts" - it floats and loses all contact with the railhead. Then the train is at the mercy of gravity which will pull it back to earth very quickly, and most likely off the railhead (and probably straight into a field).  And herein lies the problem - "techbros" enjoy an outdated mode of thinking - that "best" is simply the "fastest" and "coolest" as opposed to a system that is able to serve the most. And as documentaries like "Top Gear" have proved, a "private jet" style train is just a terrible idea for any public transport system.

This is not about reinventing trains, but supporting research from a startup business formed by students that formerly competed on a Hyperloop competition and thereby gained expertise in railroad engineering. The hope is that some of the results, be it software or parts of their technical solutions, turn out to be useful for more mundane railroad infrastructure. Whether this form of research funding that results in corporate proprietary technology is a better idea than spending tax money on research positions in academia is another story. Any investor should realize that the proposed concept is a dream castle, but personally, I do admire the engineering that goes into it.

2:22 Right. And as an engineer (who studied that kind of thing), I must add that for high speed trains: 1/ the rails are all fused together, 2/ in a curve, their is a slope between the inner rail and the outer rail such that the gravity pull looks and feels vertical inside the train for the specific speed at which the curve has to be taken. Meaning that: 1/ if you have a glass of water inside a TGV, in a curve you don't feel anything, the water level stays horizontal, and you don't see anything, but occasionally you wonder why the horizon by the window looks up like you're witnessing a mountain, while on the other side it looks down and that has no alternative explanation ; and 2/ on such a system, trains all have to drive at the exact speed the rails are built for, so no congestion is allowed and no slow-down as well...
I live in France and a system like TGV is great as is, the only thing that could be better is cost but that is to be expected as the high speed + networks is still developing in some areas. You can cross the country in 3 hours and arrive in the city centers, nothing comes even close to being as fast door to door.

As railway engineer most of the points were correct. Especially that you can't drive 500km/h or even 250km/h on existing mix-use railway due to geometry. At very high speeds you need more cant at curves and they also need to be more gentle. If cant is too high, you can't safely drive in that rail at lower speed anymore. In switches you can drive at least 200km/h when you go straight through it, but if you switch rails at there, then the speeds are way lower or switch needs to be very very long with gentle curve

I also find that Nevomo business plan bonkers. But on the technical side - one major issue of maglev is the lack of interoperability with the existing network. So it's very expensive to build and limited to point-to-point services. But if it could move between the maglev and conventional rail networks like Nevomo suggests, that might bring some benefits. Build the network piece by piece, use existing railway stations in the city centers, etc. Perhaps it will still lose compared to high speed rail due to other factors - but it would not be that ridiculous.  As for the pod concept - no idea why they push it for the passenger trains. Maglev can happily operate a full-length train sets.  The driver-less operation for shunting in terminals could work without drivers - same way shunter personnel can operate engines remotely in some places. You still have someone around, but you reduce the number of people you need. Plus you don't need the engine that constantly needs to go in, go out, switch tracks, etc. Although all this idea depends on now much it would cost... even the European Digital Automatic Coupler is getting major pushback. There is a lot of freight cars out there and fitting each and every one with propulsion would cost a fortune.

The front and back of each train, in fact, contain 360-degree panoramic windows rather than a conductor's cabin, sort of underscoring the driverless nature of the line.  I think the only reason this can exist, however, is because the Yurikamome line is entirely self-contained -- no other trains ride on those tracks, period. In fact, the Yurikamome trains use a special grade of track that's entirely unique to those trains, so no other trains even can ride on those tracks!  ...Which sort of underscores what you're saying, really, since there is literally no way to achieve this on existing tracks without creating an absolutely unconscionable safety hazard situation.

Every time a European denigrates the north american rail network, I am reminded that the North American rail network is, BY FAR, the most efficient rail freight network on the planet, being literally twice as efficient at moving freight as the EU network, moving over twice as much freight by rail per year than the entire EU despite the lower population, and moving that freight farther (with 48% of journeys exceeding 1,200 km compared to only 15% greater than 1000 km in the EU).  (Additionally north american rail freight is SO efficient that it actually produces less CO2 emisions per ton-mile than the average for waterborne freight.  Which is also 10x less emissions per ton-mile than road freight. Passenger rail, even when operating at full capacity, has a much worse margin over personal road vehicles, struggling to reach 1/3, and long distance passenger rail is significantly worse than light rail at aroun 2/3 the emissions per passenger mile, being barely better air travel)  The EU also moves over 45% of long distance (not intracity or "final mile" delivery trucks), freight by road & less than 10% by rail, (with another less than 10% being shipped by inland waterways & pipelines with the remaining ~35% being literally shipped by sea from one european port to another).  Meanwhile the USA moves 37% by rail, 31% via long haul trucks, &  32% via non-surface (with 21% of that being via pipelines).  Some of these differences are due to geography, what sort of freight is being shipped (like the USA shipping more bulk minerals by rail alone than the entire capacity of the EU transportation network), & compatibility issues integrating multiple national networks ; however, by one study I'm looking at, somewhere around 1/6 of the difference between the skewed ratio between european road & rail is directly attributable to government policy prioritizing & subsidizing passenger rail service which directly impaced the efficiency of freight service in a negative way.   TLDR.  The USA has poor intercity & Transcontinental passenger rail service compared to the EU, because the north american rail network is specialized for efficient freight trains and we use road transport for people. The EU on the otherhand crippled their rail freight services to prop up intercity passenger rail, and puts freight on the roads.  The result? The average european travels by rail 500 km more by train per year than the average US citizen but 8,000 km less total travel, spends 13% of their budget on transportation vs 16%, and pay substancially higher shipping costs.

If you think American trains are a joke, wait until you see Canadian trains. To start, we have two cross-country trains. Not two train lines, two trains. They each travel across the country once per week, taking 4 days to do so (which is worse than it was in the 90s, when it only took three days). Even with that extra leeway, the trains are still usually delayed by over 12 hours, with a punctuality rate of 8%. Economy class tickets start at $500 each way during off-peak season, and don't even include a bed or meals on a 4 day journey. If you want the sleeper cabin, which comes with meals, you could be paying as much as $2500 each way.  An equivalent airplane flight is less than 5 hours, has many flights per day, costs $100-200 each way, and is usually on time.

There is actually an area train pods make perfect sense. Yet, the tech bros aren‚Äôt interested because it isn‚Äôt flashy enough: Connecting Rural areas and small towns/cities that don‚Äôt have enough traffic/people to support a conventional trains and train routes to bigger cities but do have unused rail lines. A German company has developed a pod that uses only one of the two rails of a rail line and can safely pass each other. Sadly, rather than fill a need, the tech pros made it only have a range, so it could only be used within a city and look fancy. To them, the market for connecting cities wasn‚Äôt cool enough, so making the pods bigger or towing a rechargeable battery so it could connect cities was never considered. They did make the pods automated but this actually makes more sense going to or from small cites/towns instead of within a city because of the very low population density and thus very little chance of hitting something or someone. Here hoping they can see the profit that could be made by creating a product that fills a need not already being met.
Pods have the ~ Potential ~ to be a net improvement if done right, but can still be done with everything else being 90% the same as conventional trains.  Lets say we do eventually get a HSR somewhere in the midwest - Chicago to Denver as an example. Point to point at 300kph that's about 5 hours from downtown to downtown, which is faster than flying if you account for travel to/from airports and all the other headaches that go with it. The problem is once you start adding more cities along the way, that insist on being included to support the rail project, the total travel time becomes less feasible. So you are left with two options:  1) Shorter trip (by time) that has maybe a few stops (Say Springfield, St Louis, and Kansas City for this example)  2) stopping at all towns along the way at a cost of a few hours of delay (hard to quickly work the math out for exact numbers)  In an ideal world - all these express trains (high speed or otherwise) would have matching time tables that would get a small trains arriving before the HSR makes its stop, then leaving shortly after for anyone getting off. This gets the best of both worlds: - Short total duration to make the rail as useful as possible - Local communities are fully serviced which has massive implications for people living there, and for giving people that work in major cities an option to live far from work.  The problem is you would need to operate dozens of trains at a major loss all along this route to make this work, as you'd only be servicing a handful of people a few times per day. This is where pods can be useful ~in theory~  If you had train cars that could be operated as a regular train car, and an autonomous pod for the "last mile" of the journey, you have the capability to serve an incredibly wide network of towns, on just one line. In a fancy setup - the pods in the back of the train can split off from the main train to take passengers to a local town, and a new train can join on for new joining passengers. This of course, requires autonomous trains which is not possible as the video mentions, but by restricting a given pod to one region you can simplify the constraints  This is also why the electric train car pods have some merit - on major lines we can build out tracks with overhead power, and the locomotive can just pull from that - but on the regional lines it will take a lot more time and money to overhaul the tracks.   TLDR - if done correctly HSR with pods can reap all the benifits of traditional trains, with the ability to serve more local areas. BUT theres still some major technical and process challenges before its possible to do correctly

There is much to be said about the role of the car industry in Germany and about how shit the train punctuality is, but let's please stay factual.   Unlike France, Germany isn't a near empty desert surrounding one central mega city with some medium cities on the outermost periphery, but a relatively densely populated, yet also evenly spread out system of small, medium and larger cities. Those cities also generally have city center train stations. This means that there is a high amount of potential high speed lines that would need to be build for a separate system, which would have to compete with regular lines in general, but especially on the approaches to stations with little to no extra space being available for expansion of the latter. Now add to that the incredibly prevalent NIMBY culture (not to mention bureaucracy) which prevents anything from being built this side of 30 years and you get a system where essentially only the existing lines can maybe get upgraded (at the cost of significant disruptions of an already overtaxed system) because the state/DB already has right of way there.  I WISH the automotive industry was to blame for the German rail network, because that would mean there was an easy fix; but no, unfortunately it is the people themselves that are the problem - and the politicians, media and laws enabling the rampant NIMBYism in the first place. And - to a smaller extent - Germany's population geographics.

Obviously, hypothetical max speed and operational max speed are two very different things, but the point is we've had trains that could compete with this thing on speed for nearly 25 years and yet we havent pushed them pass 350km/h.  You've got to assume there is some pretty majorn fundamental reason for that. I'd assume the sheer cost of infastructure to support higher speeds is simply not outweighed by the reduced travel time from the higher speeds.  If your existing HSR service takes an hour to complete it's journey, then going up to 500km/h is going to save maybe 10mins? Thats cool but at what cost? Well that also means more cost building the route to the required standard simply because there is more of it to build. On the topic of people that don't understand rail thinking it's all about speed, I live in one of the few areas in the US where there is a regional rail line (Utah), and I have genuinely considered taking that train to work even though it would be up to an hour slower than driving. Why? Well because it's 1. cheaper, 2. easier, 3. safer, 4. less stressful. Literally the only thing stopping me is the lack of fast last mile transit to both my home and office, meaning that I would have to drive to the station from home, and then take a bike to my office, which doesn't look super safe on some of the roads I'd have to bike on (that and I don't own a bike atm). Notice how none of the issues keeping me from the train have to do with the speed of the train, like the utah frontrunner only runs at ~80mph, which is barely faster than the speed limit on the adjacent highway, and including stops, it's slower. But I would legitimately still take it, like for $5/day I get to sit in an air conditioned seat and glide smoothly into the city while getting work done on my laptop, whereas when driving gas alone costs $6/day if I'm lucky and I get to take a bumpy highway through a sea of other idiot drivers and have to be focused on driving the whole time, this is the tradeoff that makes me want trains, not the thrill of knowing I'm moving at over 100mph, it's not a rollercoaster, it's a convenience.

I mean, just watching the thumbnail should be enough. First, we will use a dictionary. Which the designers clearly haven't used before.  Train - "a series of connected railway carriages or wagons moved by a locomotive or by integral motors."  Calling this a "train" is just wrong to begin with. Railbus and railtruck would be more appropriate.  Second, the thing that makes actual trains better than all alternatives is that it's a train. It has modular capacity and moves all passengers and cargo in one fell swoop. The benefit is that you have less traffic on the rails. Sure, a very very long and slow train is also going to congest the lines. But traffic flow wise, trains are always better than singular vehicles.  A train's freight car can hold between 3 and 5 times as much as road going semi trucks with trailers. Meaning for every railcar, there's up to 5 trucks taken off the roads. Since trains can be as long as 100 wagons (and in certain places of the world, even longer) you can substitute 500 semi trucks with a single 100 car long train. Even if the train is moving a quarter the speed of the trucks, say 15 mph, it would still BEAT the trucks even when the trucks can make two trips for every single train trip. And it would take 500 truck drivers rather than 2-3 train engineers to do it!  So i can already tell this idea will fail for freight transport by simple math... 100 freight rail trucks means you now have 100 times as much traffic to deal with on the rails and they are suggesting we use the same rails that already exist and share space with all other rail traffic? It would be a NIGHTMARE! If they connected each rail truck together into a single, actual, train then they would at least not have that failing point.  And that, as far as freight goes, is long before the other huge benefit to freight rail transport... You have one or a few locomotives for every large number of wagons. This is a maintenance issue, as maintaining the locomotive is VERY expensive compared to a freight car. If every freight car is its own locomotive you now have to pay maintenance on every vehicle by its own. Which will be EXPENSIVE and thus lose out to traditional rail freight in terms of running costs. There are no benefits to having single carriages that could possibly outweigh the low cost of running a single engine train. And traditional trains DO split up and join, so any argument that smaller "packets" on the rail network is a selling point is MOOT. As trains already do that, each wagon is a single "packet" in the rail network and is dropped off at the nearest hub to be picked up by a train going towards its final destination. Rail freight transport is indirect and slow because it's CHEAPER that way, there's no rush. And if there is a rush, then a train that goes straight from source to destination STILL outcompetes trucks in transport costs. It's the same reason we don't have container ships doing 70 knots across the high seas. Every knot above optimal cruising speed is MONEY spent. And container ships operate in one of the tightest margins known to transport kind, you can send a container full of small packages where the shipping cost of each package becomes as low as 0.01 cents. No matter the distance traveled! There is simply nothing to gain by being fast or going for express A->B transport when delivery can wait.  As for railbuses, aka passenger transport in single carriage self propelled rail vehicles, it still doesn't make sense. You still run into the congestion issue that is solved by connecting several carriages together. And traditional passenger trains already do this, and split as necessary to have diverging end points on the rail network.  There is simply NO benefits to having single unit self propelled vehicles on rails. Which also applies to highways by the way. A single high capacity bus that carries 50 passengers going to the same places will replace 50 obnoxious SUV's with a single occupant in them. Imagine this for a second... If you are stuck in traffic, and everyone on the road morphed into buses, there would be one bus for every 50 cars. Traffic would flow as smooth as if there wasn't traffic at all! You could reduce a ten lane highway to single lanes each direction and there would still be a FIFTH as many vehicles in each lane!  Next time you are sitting in traffic, think about that... The FIFTY cars ahead of you would simply DISAPPEAR and a bus would pop into existence.  Small capacity vehicles are STUPID in any mode of transport!

The whole "high speed cargo" thing is asinine, because in most cases the actual bottleneck in freight is the end-point freight yards themselves. Sure get the trains to go 500km/h whatever, but if the freight yard can only move a set amount of containers per day, and only send out a set number of trucks per day, then speeding up the actual travel time of rail lines is just going to give them a larger back log if they're already operating near capacity.

I'm from this country you may have heard of called Canada. And we have this type of metro train in Vancouver that uses linear induction motors to propel the train forward along the track, while using normal rails to carry the trains weight and guide it and a normal third rail for traction power. Basically a linear induction motor (which is how maglevs propel) unrolls an electric motor and splits it between an electromagnet on the train bogie and a continuous one on the tracks. Alternating postive and negative magnetic fields 'sucks' the train along the tracks. Though LIM trains can take steeper hills and accelerate marginally faster, they have basically the same performance specs as traditional electric traction motor metro trains.

Not again! Another bullshit idea to siphon some money from some EU project or gullible investors, probably.  The main disadvantage of trains is that they only go on the tracks they have... So if you live in town A and want to go to vacation in a village 10 km of town B (or commute daily to work in the other side of town B than the train station), you need a reasonable connection (public transport, or coach service). That's why the punctuality and reliability is paramount.  If I go on vacation once in a longer time, I don't care much if the train ride takes 4 or 8 hours, as long as I can plan for it and book appropriate connection. If it's about the daily commute (e.g. 50km), it does not matter much if it takes 20 minutes or 5 (I can read a book, or organize my notes for the meeting or whatever... it's not a wasted time anyway), and again, I much more need that it comes at the right time + that there is enough capacity, if there are many people taking the same route (if there are not, then nobody is going to invest in building it anyway). So where would make most sense to build something would be some short commuter distances ... but wait, for these it's good when there are enough stops so that the people who use it daily have it close by to their homes and offices.   So again, the ultra-speed is useless, because it would speed up and brake all the time anyway, never reaching the top speed (and you don't want the commuter train to get many Gs of acceleration, do you?).  What we need is to improve reliability and coverage, not speed (and definitely not speed on "pods" that are not coupled together and can't take many passengers).  Combining the cons of trains (need for tracks) with the cons of cars (low capacity) is not the way to go...

Something else worth pointing out to compliment the point that speed isn't everything and frequency is just as important is that if trains run too fast, you won't have as much frequency! Fast trains means they take longer to stop, which means you need more spacing between the signalling beacons and posts, which means you can't run as many trains as you could with a 300/320km/h high speed rail system which can theoretically offer up to 20 trains per hour running them as little as three minutes apart. Not that you would need that many in reality, but still. I think the industry has already shown that 300/320km/h is the sweet spot for high speed with maybe 350km/h if you really need to squeeze that speed and capacity like in Japan and China.

The last point is the most important one. What prevents trains going faster isn't their propulsion system, it's their track geometry. If a high speed line is designed for speeds of 300km/h, the curves on the line will mean that a train cannot (comfortably) go above that speed. Regarding propulsion, a souped-up TGV once got up to 574km/h on a standard high speed line, but, as you spoke about here, it flew up a lot of ballast in the process. The point is that the fancy propulsion system isn't even needed because it is possible to reach such high speeds through more conventional, proven (and crucially cheaper) methods.

I'm so confused - it is not a pod. It has too many seats. The idea behind pods is that you have offline stations and the pod doesn't stop anywhere you aren't trying to go, also the pod seats only 4 people because the idea of a pod is to eliminate both (1) waiting for your train to arrive, since you just step into an empty pod already parked and waiting at an offline station, and (2) waiting at a station that is not your destination, as the central computer drives your pod directly from your origin to destination. Why make a big pod and then run it on regular tracks that pass through the station? Why not just use a regular train in that case? The whole point of pods is to make it so a stopped pod is not holding up traffic.

You're a good bit off counting the passenger capacity. If a train transports 400 passengers between points A and B then back to A in 4 hours, at 100km/h, you don't need 20 20-passenger pods to have the same capacity, just 5, as each pod will complete a round-trip in 1 hour, meaning transport 80 passengers in 4 hours, not 20. Plus train capacity is nowadays rarely fully utilized and even disregarding travel length, long time between departures creates serious inconvenience. And for cargo, you're completely missing mail. Something that shouldn't have broken broke and the production line is stopped. You need a replacement part ASAP. You order it from a manufacturer in Turkey, they get it on an airplane in the afternoon, it delivers your order to nearest cargo terminal in 6 hours, but your town doesn't have an airport, so the goods will go by truck or by mail train in the morning, to arrive tomorrow and be delivered at workday's end, with $1mln in lost profits. Or you pay extra, they get sent by the pod, and you'll have them in the morning, and you'll fix the line by 9AM.

The most hilrious thing to me is:  Modern TGV and ICE Models are capable of going way past what they are driving at. The TGV record is 572 km/h. It is logistics, as well as most simple logical issues, which makes it unviable to have trains going faster than now.  Even on the better highspeed tracks, it simply doesn't make sense to go way faster as right now, as either you would have to completely redistribute traffic for some few trains to exclusively ride their super-highspeed with longer, uninterrupted distances between stations, or -even on the longest current highspeed tracks - the trains would have to accelerate and decelerate so quickly to actually reach those speeds, you would have to install a luggage system, seat belt and check-up system akin to planes. As a german I can say, yes, punctuality and frequency are the main reasons I don't like taking trains. In order to truly compete with cars, you should not have to check any schedule/app/whatever, if you get to a station there should be a train that goes in your wanted direction within 5 minutes. And of course it would have to be cheaper.  Call me a tech bro but there should not be any on-site employee involved in driving/attending trains. The driver is a glorified emergency brake button pusher, a minimally educated passenger can sit at the front and do that. The attendants are glorified ticket machines, also replaceable, ideally replaced by nothing (free public transport, please!). Trains could also drive far closer behind each other if they used 1000$/train of 21st century tech, also making physical train signals and their maintenance costs obsolete. How? Each meter of train tracks (from the entire train network) can be reserved by a train (using a central server). Your train can only (automatically) drive up to the speed that allows it to still stop within its reserved track positions. GPS+Mobile data should be enough to make it happen. What if communication is interrupted? Can't reserve new positions, so train stops safely. Likely the communication is restored before the train comes to a stop because it is still moving, leaving the spot where the connection is bad. What if GPS is interrupted? The computer can determine the position using other cheap sensors (speedometer+accelerometer), accuracy might be degraded but it would only result in more needed reservations for a given speed to account for deviations, so just a bit more separation between trains.

the funniest bit about replacing trains by pods is that the limiting factor with trains is mainly how many trains you can get on a single pair of rails.  The north-eastern entry to the Bern main station has 4 rails, and during peak hours has a train going through every minute, translating to a train every 4 minutes for each rail. that is pretty much maximum capacity.  now imagine what would happen if you replaced a significant part of these trains, which are mostly over a hundred metres long, with pods. you can't really get more than one of them per minute through for safety reasons. There is a great misunderstanding of the topic here. Modern technology has no major problems to build "trains" that travel faster than 500 km/h. The problem is not the cost, the construction of tracks for 300+ km/h commercial trains and for 500+ km/h maglev trains is very similar The problem is that you can't send one trains in to other trains' tracks. It's like building a motorway for special cars that can't drive on normal roads. A solution is being sought, is like as the motorways that are currently in operation, where on the same road you have a truck at 80 km/h and a sports car at 400 km/h (in Germany). And both can travel on local roads, even when the speed limit is 30.
It's not really true that you can't have driverless trains.  There are many completed automated metro systems in use in the real world today.  What they do require is purpose-built, completely grade-separated and isolated tracks.  And typically things like platform screen doors as well to ensure no passengers ever fall on the tracks.  So while the whole idea of retrofitting old rail lines for this is impractical, I do want to push back against saying that you can't have automated trains.  You not only can, many places already do.

The story about maglev is actually pretty sad. I had to work through the engineers recommendations written for politicians around the test project they did in germany decades ago. Basically the project should've been a success, maglev trains have tons of advantages over ICEs: Less Cost, Power Use, Noise, wildlife disruption etc. Even interoperability between regular rail and maglev was in development. However all these advantages only exist on long tracks - and at ICE speeds. Let me repeat that: Maglev are better than ICEs if they are on long tracks and operate at about the same speeds.  However at the same time, european politicians, especially france and germany had a dick measuring contest to build the fastest land travel, which ruined the maglev project and lost to the TGV anyway, all to close the "speed gap" between trains and plains some "brilliant marketing genius" invented.

We in czech republic have a problem with train operators. They are underpaid, overworked and they work for several companies to catch up. There are also rail operators from foreign countries, who were not educated for czech railways, as we have different systems. Plus the companies send the most expendable workers to the far eastern tracks. This issue is worsened by the fact that one national company was fractioned into several pieces, and other companies were allowed to "partake" in the business. Which means they get all the opportunities but no responsibilities, making the companies profitable. The partitioned national railway operator meanwhile struggles with internal corruption due to restructuralisation, meaning the company¬¥s funds are alocated in company¬¥s management, instead of employee wages and modernisation. The accident on the picture was caused by human error, of overworked train operator, and worsened by te fact, that private company operates wagons so structuraly weak, they crumble like a fcking crouton, with passegers still inside. This would not be the case, if the one national operator used only one kind of wagons, produced at home, instead of obsolete repainted german scrap metal.

German here. The car industry isnt a major factor in our railways demise. The car industry has been here since forever and up until the mid to late 90s the german railway system was a thing to be proud of and one of the very best in the entire world. Then privatization came. Profits were suddenly the only thing to care about so everything unprofitable went away. A lot of traintracks were simply cut and the rest had to operate as efficiently as possible. And with very tight margins comes room for spiraling errors as explained. Today the railsystem is a shadow of its former self and its almost impossible to bring it back to former glory if the money for that has been going into private pockets of the very rich.
